<p>In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, especially in the major cities, people take turns to set aside a time for <i>open house</i> when they stay at home to receive and entertain neighbours, family and other visitors. It is common to see non-Muslims made welcome during Eid at these <i>open houses</i>. They also celebrate by lighting traditional <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_cannon" title="Bamboo cannon">bamboo cannon</a> <a href="/wiki/Firecracker" title="Firecracker">firecrackers</a> known as <i>meriam buluh</i>, using <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> in large hollow <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> tubes or Chinese imported crackers. The traditional bamboo cannon, <i>meriam bambu</i>, and fireworks are notoriously loud and can be very dangerous to operator, bystander and even nearby buildings. These are usually bamboo tubes 5–10&#160;cm (2.0–3.9&#160;in) in diameter and 4–7&#160;m (13–23&#160;ft) long, filled with either: water and several hundred grams of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbide" title="Calcium carbide">calcium carbide</a>, or heated kerosene, then ignited by match.
</p><p>Celebrating with crackers in the early morning during Ramadan is now banned in many areas.
</p><p>In Malaysia, children are given token sums of money, also known as "duit raya", from their parents or elders.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/The_Philippines" class="mw-redirect" title="The Philippines">the Philippines</a>, Eid al-Fitr, known to Muslims as "Araw ng Lebaran" / "Araw ng Raya" or familiar to the Christian majority and other non-Muslims as "<i>Wakás ng Ramadán</i>" ("End of Ramadan") or incorrectly as "<i>Ramadan</i>", has been recognised by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Philippines" title="Government of the Philippines">Philippine Government</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_Philippines" title="Public holidays in the Philippines">regular holiday</a> by virtue of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Philippine_laws" title="List of Philippine laws">Republic Act</a> No. 9177 and it is also according to Presidential Proclamation No. 1083, signed into law on 13 November 2002&#160;– the only majority Christian country worldwide to have done so.
</p><p>The law was enacted in deference to the <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Philippines" title="Islam in the Philippines">Filipino Muslim</a> community and to promote peace and harmony among major religions in the country. The first national commemoration of Eid al-Fitr was on 6 December 2002, marked by prayers and feasting. Some Filipino Muslims attend grand congregations at the <a href="/wiki/Masjid_Al-Dahab" title="Masjid Al-Dahab">Manila Golden Mosque</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Luneta_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Luneta Park">Quirino Grandstand</a> every Eid, while Muslim-majority communities in <a href="/wiki/Mindanao" title="Mindanao">Mindanao</a> stage large public celebrations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Myanmar">Myanmar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Myanmar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Eid al-Fitar lasts for only one day among <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam in Burma">Burmese Muslims</a>, who call the day <i>Eid Nei’</i> (Nei’=day) or <i>Eid Ka Lay</i> (Ka Lay=small) or <i>Shai Mai Eid</i> (Shai Mai=a meal of sweet vermicelli served with fried cashews, coconut shreds, raisins, and milk that is traditionally eaten by Burmese Muslims during Eid). Burmese Muslims predominantly follow the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school of jurisprudence in <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a>.
</p><p>During Ramadan, in the small towns and big villages with significant Muslim populations, Burmese Muslim youth organise singing teams called Jago (in Urdu and Hindi), which means "wake up". Jago teams usually do not use musical instruments apart from the occasional use of harmonica mouth organs.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> These youths will walk throughout the neighbourhoods before sunrise to wake up the fellow Muslims for Suhoor (pre-dawn meal), which precedes the day of fasting.
</p><p>The roving groups of singers will take the tunes of popular Hindi movie songs, replaced with Burmese lyrics and invocations about fasting, the do's and don'ts of Islam and about the benefits of Salaat.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> These songs could also be called <i><a href="/wiki/Qawwali" title="Qawwali">Qawwali</a></i>, which are popular in India and Pakistan. Sometimes these Jago groups will also visit Muslim homes on the Eid day, where they are welcomed with food and monetary donations for the team with Eidi or Duit Raya.
</p><p>Although Eid al-Fitr is not a <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Public holidays in Burma">public holiday in Burma</a>, most employers have an understanding of the festival and are usually willing to accommodate days off for Muslim staff. Some may even take time off during office hours to visit with Muslim staff at their homes, usually accompanied by other non-Muslim co&#160;– workers. As there is no single Islamic authority in Burma to make official decisions on moon-sighting, it is sometimes difficult to reach consensus on the start and end of Ramadan. This often results in Eid being celebrated on different days in small towns and villages.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a> "Festival of Sacrifice" or "Greater Eid" is a public holiday in Burma as this event falls annually on the 10th day of the month of <a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a> (ذو الحجة) in the lunar <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a>. Unlike Muslim countries that observe a three-day festival, Eid al-Adha is only observed on one day in Burma. During both Eids, the traditional greeting is merely the common Islamic greeting of <i>Assalamualaikum</i>, and <i>Eid Mubarak</i> is only seldom heard. The greeting is followed by placing the right hand on the forehead (as if giving a salute); there is no shaking of hands and rarely only includes a formal embrace.
</p><p>Gifts and food are frequently given to the elder relatives and even to non-Muslim employers and government authorities. New clothes are traditionally given to family members and co&#160;– workers, but Burmese Muslims elders will give Eidi gifts to children. Children will receive at least token amounts of money, even from strangers, especially if they went around the neighbourhoods in groups just to collect Eidi. It is common for children and young people to go around giving greetings of "salaam" to parents, elder relatives and other elders in the community. During Eid, Burmese Muslims ask forgiveness from parents and elders and themselves try to forgive and forget any misunderstandings that may have occurred amongst one another.
</p><p>Sometimes Burmese Muslims pray or perform Eid salah (called Eid Namaz) at an Eidgah in open spaces outdoors. Burmese Muslim women typically do not attend the mosque or join with the men at an Eidgah.
</p><p>As Burmese Muslims are discouraged by the religious authorities from decorating their homes with lights, lamps or colourful bulbs, sending Eid cards, and more recently, sending e-cards through the internet, is fairly common. Children and adults are also urged not to celebrate any religious festival with fireworks or firecrackers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">Islam in China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hui_family_eid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hui_family_eid.jpg/220px-Hui_family_eid.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hui_family_eid.jpg/330px-Hui_family_eid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hui_family_eid.jpg/440px-Hui_family_eid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="332" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hui_family_eid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ethnic <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui</a> family celebrating Eid al-Fitr in <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the People's Republic of China, out of 56 officially recognised ethnic groups, Eid al-Fitr is celebrated by at least 10 ethnic groups that are predominantly Muslim. These groups are said to total 18 million according to official statistics, but some observers say the actual number may be much higher. It is also a <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Public holidays in the People&#39;s Republic of China">public holiday in China</a> in certain regions, including two Province Prefecture Level regions, <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">Ningxia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>. All residents in these areas, regardless of religion, are entitled to either a one-day or three-day official holiday. Outside the Muslim-majority regions, only Muslims are entitled to a one-day holiday. In Xinjiang province, Eid al-Fitr is even celebrated by <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> population during which holiday supplies of mutton, lamb and beef are distributed to households as part of welfare programme funded by government agencies, public and private institutions, and businesses.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, Muslim populations are spread throughout the region. On Eid al-Fitr, however, some devotees may travel to <a href="/wiki/Sayyid_Ajjal_Shams_al-Din_Omar" class="mw-redirect" title="Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar">Sayyid 'Ajjal</a>'s grave after their communal prayers. There, they will conduct readings from the Quran and clean the tomb, reminiscent of the historic annual Chinese Qingming festival, in which people go to their ancestors' graves, sweep and clean the area and make food offerings.
</p><p>Finally the accomplishments of the Sayyid 'Ajall will be related in story form, concluded by a special prayer service to honour the hundreds of thousands of Muslims killed during the <a href="/wiki/Panthay_Rebellion" title="Panthay Rebellion">Panthay Rebellion</a>, and the hundreds killed during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Eid al-Fitr (i.e. Seker Bayram, Sugar Feast) is celebrated in Greece mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">Western Thrace</a> region from the local <a href="/wiki/Muslim_minority_of_Greece" title="Muslim minority of Greece">Muslim minority</a> (<a href="/wiki/Turks_of_Western_Thrace" title="Turks of Western Thrace">Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomaks" title="Pomaks">Pomaks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romani_people_in_Greece" title="Romani people in Greece">Roma</a>), along with the other two major celebrations, <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Kurban Bayram</a> (Sacrifice Feast) and <a href="/wiki/H%C4%B1d%C4%B1rellez" title="Hıdırellez">Hıdırellez</a>.
On the day of the Bayram, family gathers together, wears its best clothes, and celebrate with a common meal, after having attend the morning prayer. The women prepare and offer sweets to family and visitors, while small children go around and pay their respects to the elderly, by kissing their hands, and they in turn reward them with caddies, sweets, and small amount of money.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-edu_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
Local Muslim shopkeepers close their shops this day, while <a href="/wiki/Muslim_minority_in_Greece#Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim minority in Greece">Muslim minority schools</a> have a 5-day holiday for the feast.<sup id="cite_ref-edu_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edu-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Some entertainment venues and clubs hold special events for the night of the Bayram.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Australia, a predominantly secular country, Muslims are able to practise their religion with great freedom. Most large companies<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> allow for special religious holidays allowing Muslims to take a day off for Eid al-Fitr. Areas where there are large (but not necessarily majority) Muslim populations have overflowing attendances at the mosque for the Eid al-Fitr prayer. Police frequently block off roads and divert traffic to cater for the prayer and subsequent festivities. Eid prayers are also held in open areas (playground, stadium) in some places.
</p><p>In 1987, The Australian MEFF Consortium commenced the Multicultural Eid Festival and Fair<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> to celebrate Eid in Sydney, held shortly after Eid al-Fitr. The festival has grown to now cater for tens of thousands of Muslims and non-Muslims and has included as guests <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Yusuf islam">Yusuf Islam</a>, famous Australian rugby player, <a href="/wiki/Hazem_El-Masri" class="mw-redirect" title="Hazem El-Masri">Hazem El Masri</a>, the then governor-general of Australia, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jeffery" title="Michael Jeffery">Michael Jeffery</a> and the previous premier of New South Wales, <a href="/wiki/Kristina_Keneally" title="Kristina Keneally">Kristina Keneally</a>. This festival has now been replicated in cities all around Australia. The biggest Eid fair in Melbourne is held in <i>Broadmeadows</i> usually on the weekend following the Eid day. In Canberra, the capital of Australia, Eid Festival sponsored by Australian Federal Police (AFP) is held on the Sunday after the Eid day. The festival includes stalls from different nations, cultural programme, and rides for kids and adults.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most Muslims in the United States offer the Eid prayer in big-city Islamic centers, convention halls or open parks. Muslims from different cultures with multi-national customs get together for prayers and celebrations. In some cities, prayers are done at multiple times to accommodate the large number of attendees. Generally, Muslims visit each other's homes on Eid or hold large feasts in mosques or community halls. Sometimes, mosques rent parks for Muslims to pray in.  Women and children may adorn their hands with henna to mark the celebration.  Typically, new clothing and attire are worn.  Gifts are often exchanged amongst children.  Another ritual or practice is the giving of 'Eidee', usually a nominal amount of a cash gift to children or youth to mark the occasion.
</p><p>During the 3 days of Eid, many Muslims join big parties sponsored either by a community mosque or Islamic center or by a wealthy Muslim in the community. Children receive gifts, and all participants enjoy sweet, spicy and other flavourful delicacies. Many Muslims also donate money to those less fortunate. Sometimes, Muslims reserve amusement parks, skating rinks or other activity centers for an entire day of fun.
</p><p>In New York City alternate side parking (street cleaning) regulations are suspended. Beginning in 2016, New York City Public Schools will also remain closed on Eid.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
In <a href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Houston, Texas">Houston, Texas</a>, the annual prayers are offered at the <a href="/wiki/George_R._Brown_Convention_Center" title="George R. Brown Convention Center">George R. Brown Convention Center</a> in downtown Houston, organised by the Islamic Society of Greater Houston (ISGH).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> (USPS) has issued several Eid postage stamps, across several years&#160;– starting in 2001&#160;– honoring "two of the most important festivals in the Islamic calendar: Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha." Eid stamps were released in 2001–2002, 2006–2009, and a <a href="/wiki/Forever_stamp" class="mw-redirect" title="Forever stamp">Forever® stamp</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin:0 auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1208px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>, New York, United States of America</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For Eid al-Fitr, just as in the United States, most Canadian Muslims will take a day off from work and go to prayers held in big-city mosques or Islamic centres, convention halls or sports arenas. Muslims from different cultures with multi-national customs get together for prayers and celebrations. In the larger cities of Toronto, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, Vancouver, <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, congregational prayers may be done at multiple times to accommodate the large number of attendees. Many Muslims will visit each other's homes on the Eid day or the days following to attend designated "open houses" in which everyone is welcome to visit. Children receive gifts or money, and sweets and tasty dishes are served throughout the day. Smaller Muslim communities, particularly in the rural areas, hold other communal gatherings in mosques or rented community halls. Muslims also donate money or contribute to their local food banks on this day for those who are less fortunate.
</p><p>In many Canadian communities, Muslim organisations and mosques also hold large Eid parties that are open to the entire Muslim community. Some groups may reserve amusement parks or other activity centres for an entire day of fun and celebration, while others may hold public Eid parties in mosques as a means of outreach to the larger non-Muslim society.
</p><p>Students from Canadian schools may take 2–3 days off, because Eid is a major holiday in the Islamic culture.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinidad_and_Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Trinidad and Tobago">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Eid-al-Fitr is a public Holiday in Trinidad and Tobago.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations,_1941_D5129.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg/170px-Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg/255px-Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg/340px-Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations%2C_1941_D5129.jpg 2x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslims_in_Britain-_Eid_Ul_Fitr_Celebrations,_1941_D5129.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muslims in Britain performing the <a href="/wiki/Eid_prayers" title="Eid prayers">Eid prayers</a> during the celebration, 1941</div></div></div>
<p>Although Eid al-Fitr is not a recognised public holiday in the United Kingdom, many schools, businesses and organisations allow for at least a day's leave to be taken for religious celebrations.
</p><p>During the morning, observant men usually wear a <i><a href="/wiki/Thawb" title="Thawb">thawb</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jubba" class="mw-redirect" title="Jubba">jubba</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Sherwani" title="Sherwani">sherwani</a></i>, and women usually wear a <i><a href="/wiki/Salwar_kameez" class="mw-redirect" title="Salwar kameez">salwar kameez</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Abaya" title="Abaya">abaya</a></i> or any other traditional clothing. Generally speaking, men, women and children will wear their best clothes. They will then proceed to a local mosque, community centre or park (in the summer months) for the Eid prayer. During the journey to the mosque, and up until the start of the prayer, it is Islamic tradition to recite <i><a href="/wiki/Takbeer" class="mw-redirect" title="Takbeer">takbeer</a></i>&#160;– a reminder that God is Greater. Immediately after the Eid prayer and sermon have finished, people greet each other with "Eid Mubarak," or the equivalent in their mother-tongue. Some men may go to a local cemetery after Eid prayers to remember the deceased and pray for them. When they return home they will congratulate family and friends and other Muslims, before having breakfast together of traditional sweet and savoury treats. Gifts and money are usually given to children.
</p><p>Throughout the day, everyone will either visit or host friends and relatives, sharing some of the traditional foods with them. <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshi_cuisine" title="Bangladeshi cuisine">Bangladeshi dishes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani food">Pakistani dishes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Samosa" title="Samosa">samosas</a>, <i>Siwey</i>a, <i>Rice and Handesh</i>, <i>Noonor Bora</i>, and <i>Fulab</i> are particularly popular within those communities. Other communities enjoy a range of traditional foods too.
</p><p>As in Egypt, there have been a small number of reports of sexual assaults associated with the Eid in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiji">Fiji</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Fiji">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Muslims comprise around 7% (63,000 people) of the total population of <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, a small tropical island-nation northeast of Australia. The Muslim community mostly consists of people of Indian origin, descendants of <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured servant">indentured labourers</a> who were brought to the islands in the late 19th and early 20th centuries by the British colonists. Although no accurate statistical evidence exists, there are also thought to be a few hundred indigenous Fijian Muslims (<a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a>) in the island nation. The vast majority of Muslims in Fiji are of the <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> branch of Islam who follow the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school of jurisprudence.
</p><p>The day of Eid al-Fitr is celebrated in Fiji with Muslim men wearing their best clothes and attending the mosque for the early morning congregational prayer (women do not go to the mosques for prayers in most parts of Fiji). This is followed by visiting relatives and neighbours. Children receive presents and money from elder members of the family, relatives and neighbours. Most Muslims will wear new clothes on this day, and serve <i>samai</i>, a dish of fine, sweet <a href="/wiki/Vermicelli" title="Vermicelli">vermicelli</a> noodles mixed in warm milk. This is usually accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Samosas" class="mw-redirect" title="Samosas">samosas</a>, curried chicken and beef as well as sweets and Indian snacks for guests visiting throughout the day.
</p><p>The traditional Eid greeting is <i>Eid Mubarak</i>, and it is frequently followed by a formal embrace.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mauritius">Mauritius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Mauritius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mauritius is a diverse island nation where several religions live together in relative harmony. Muslims make up about 16.6% of the total population and Eid is one of the island's national holidays. Eid itself is celebrated across the island, with the preparation of a feast, which typically includes the "biryani". Men accomplish their Eid prayer at the local mosques or at the Eid Gah. Cultural shows are usually performed in the days that follow Eid.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Gregorian_calendar">In the Gregorian calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eid_al-Fitr&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: In the Gregorian calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a></div>
<p>Although the date of Eid al-Fitr is always the same in the Islamic calendar, the date in the Gregorian calendar falls approximately 11 days earlier each successive year, since the Islamic calendar is <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar</a> and the Gregorian calendar is <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar</a>. Hence if the Eid falls in the first ten days of a Gregorian calendar year, there will be a second Eid in the last ten days of the same Gregorian calendar year, as happened in 2000 CE. The Gregorian date may vary between countries depending on the local sightability of the new moon. Some expatriate Muslim communities follow the dates as determined for their home country, while others follow the local dates of their country of residence.
</p><p><br />
